reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a 2D transposed convolution operation with asymmetric input and square kernel, supporting dilation, padding, and stride.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the convolution kernel (square, e.g., 3 for a 3x3 kernel).
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int, optional): Padding applied to the input. Defaults to 0.
        dilation (int, optional): Spacing between kernel elements. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, dilation: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose2d = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the 2D transposed convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, height_in, width_in). 

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, height_out, width_out).
        """
        return self.conv_transpose2d(x)


# Test code
batch_size = 16
in_channels = 32
out_channels = 64
kernel_size = 3
height_in = 64
width_in = 128
stride = 5
padding = 1
dilation = 2

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height_in, width_in)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, dilation]

Actually because patches don't overlap in spatial domain, each output pixel is determined by at most one input pixel coordinate (h_in, w_in) and one kernel offset (kh,kw). But there are still 32 c_in contributions that add channel-wise. So output pixel = sum_{c_in} weight[c_in, c_out, kh, kw] * input[n, c_in, h_in, w_in]. The forward computation for convtranspose2d is equivalent to conv2d on dilated input with weight transposed between in_channels and out_channels. Specifically, given input X of shape (N, in_c, H, W), we create X_dilated by inserting stride-1 zeros between elements along H and W. In convtranspose2d, we can think of gradient of conv2d wrt input. So if we imagine a conv2d with weight W^T (i.e., shape (out_c, in_c, K, K)), stride = given stride, padding = given padding, dilation = given dilation. Then the backward wrt input of that conv2d is convtranspose2d forward (without output_padding). The backward formula for conv2d wrt input is exactly a conv2d of output_grad with weight rotated 180 degrees (i.e., flipped both spatial dims) and transposed input/output channels, using stride=1, padding = kernel_pad, dilation = given dilation. Alternatively, we can think of the whole operation as convolution with stride=5, kernel=3, dilation=2, no output padding. Because stride > dilation*(K-1) and no overlap, the output is a "dilated" arrangement of patches. This is similar to DepthToSpace or PixelShuffle but with learned weights. Let's think about the most efficient kernel architecture. Observation: Since the operation is essentially a transposed convolution with stride > effective kernel size, there is no overlap between output patches from neighboring input pixels. This is equivalent to a "sub-pixel convolution" where each input pixel generates a (out_c, KH, KW) block. Wait each input pixel x[n, c_in, h, w] is associated with weights that map c_in to all out_c for each spatial offset. The total output at spatial offset (kh,kw) for output channel c_out is sum over c_in of weight[c_in, c_out, kh, kw] * x[n, c_in, h_in, w_in] where h_in and w_in are determined. Thus we can reinterpret the operation as follows: For each (kh,kw), define Y_{kh,kw}[n, c_out, h_i, w_i] = sum_{c_in} W_{kh,kw}[c_in, c_out] * X[n, c_in, h_i, w_i] where X is a cropped version of input depending on (kh,kw). Then Y is placed at output positions: Y_{kh,kw}[n, c_out, h_i, w_i] goes to output[n, c_out, stride*h_i + kh*dilation - padding, stride*w_i + kw*dilation - padding]. Since the placement has stride spacing, we can think of reshaping output channels to introduce spatial dimensions: out_c = 64, KH=3, KW=3. But out_c is arbitrary and weight shape is (in_c, out_c, KH, KW). If out_c were divisible by KH*KW, this would be exactly sub-pixel convolution (depth-to-space). But here out_c=64, KH*KW=9, 64 not divisible by 9. So can't directly map. However, we can treat each group (kh,kw) independently. Then we need to write this output matrix into the output tensor at strided positions. Since H_g*W_g per group is moderate: max for group (1,1): H_g=64, W_g=128 => 8192. Times batch 16 => 131,072. Another idea: reshape the input and output to make the operation a dense GEMM. Because stride > dilation*(K-1), there is a one-to-one mapping between input pixels and output blocks. We can view each input pixel as a vector of length in_c (32). We want to produce an output block for each pixel of shape (out_c, KH, KW). The mapping is linear: output_block = x_pixel (length 32) * W (shape [32, 64*9]). Then we reshape output_block into (64, 3, 3) and place it into the output at the appropriate location. Since the placements don't overlap, we can compute all output blocks as a single large GEMM of shape (N*H_in*W_in, 32) x (32, 64*9) = (131072, 32) x (32, 576). That's a GEMM! The resulting matrix of shape (131072, 576) can then be scattered into output. Specifically: - Input matrix X_mat shape (N*H_in*W_in, in_c). We need to place them into output positions: out[n, c_out, h_in*stride + kh*dilation - padding, w_in*stride + kw*dilation - padding]. This is a depth-to-space-like operation. Let's design the kernel signature: ``` void conv_transpose2d_no_overlap( const float* input, // (N, H, W, C_in) NHWC const float* weight, // (KH, KW, C_in, C_out) contiguous float* output, // (N, C_out, H_out, W_out) with NHWC strides int N, int H_in, int W_in, int C_in, int KH, int KW, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int H_out, int W_out) ``` Grid: dim3 grid(N, H_in, W_in) — using 3D grid. Block: dim3 block(C_out, 1, 1) — 64 threads. Each block handles one (n, h, w) pixel. Shared memory: - float smem_x[32]; // C_in - float smem_w[32*64]; // C_in*C_out for one (kh,kw) Weight for a specific (kh,kw) is at offset `((kh * KW + kw) * C_in * C_out)` in the weight array. So we load `C_in * C_out` floats starting from that offset. Since we have 64 threads, we can load the weight slice cooperatively: ``` int total_w = C_in * C_out; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < total_w; i += blockDim.x) { smem_w[i] = weight[weight_offset + i]; } ``` If `blockDim.x = 64`, then each warp loads contiguous floats. Since blockDim.x < total_w (2048), we loop with step 64. This is coalesced because consecutive threads load consecutive memory addresses. Good. Similarly, load input: ``` for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C_in; i += blockDim.x) { int input_idx = ((n * H_in + h) * W_in + w) * C_in + i; smem_x[i] = input[input_idx]; } ``` Coalesced because threads load consecutive c_in. Now, writing the CUDA kernel using `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. We need to provide `cpp_sources` with a wrapper function and `cuda_sources` with the kernel. Let's draft the kernel. The kernel: ```cuda template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void conv_transpose2d_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int H_in, int W_in, int C_in, int KH, int KW, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int H_out, int W_out, int C_out) { int n = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.z; int c_out = threadIdx.x; if (n >= N || h >= H_in || w >= W_in || c_out >= C_out) return; // Load input pixel channel values into shared memory extern __shared__ char smem[]; scalar_t* smem_x = reinterpret_cast<scalar_t*>(smem); int spatial_in = ((n * H_in + h) * W_in + w) * C_in; for (int i = c_out; i < C_in; i += blockDim.x) { smem_x[i] = input[spatial_in + i]; } __syncthreads(); // Loop over kernel spatial positions for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { int h_out = h * stride + kh * dilation - padding; if (h_out < 0 || h_out >= H_out) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { int w_out = w * stride + kw * dilation - padding; if (w_out < 0 || w_out >= W_out) continue; scalar_t acc = 0.0f; int w_base = ((kh * KW + kw) * C_in) * C_out; for (int c = 0; c < C_in; ++c) { acc += smem_x[c] * weight[w_base + c * C_out + c_out]; } int out_idx = ((n * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out) * C_out + c_out; output[out_idx] = acc; } } } ``` Wait, we need `__shared__` memory. Since blockDim.x may be > C_in, the loop for loading smem_x uses `c_out` as start. It's fine. But we need to know the size of shared memory per block: `C_in * sizeof(scalar_t)`. We can pass it to the kernel launch. Host wrapper: ```cpp torch::Tensor conv_transpose2d_forward( torch::Tensor input, // (N, H, W, C_in) NHWC torch::Tensor weight, // (KH, KW, C_in, C_out) int stride, int padding, int dilation, int H_out, int W_out) { int N = input.size(0); int H_in = input.size(1); int W_in = input.size(2); int C_in = input.size(3); int KH = weight.size(0); int KW = weight.size(1); int C_out = weight.size(3); auto options = torch::TensorOptions().dtype(input.dtype()).device(input.device()); // Output shape (N, C_out, H_out, W_out) but NHWC strides torch::Tensor output = torch::zeros_strided({N, C_out, H_out, W_out}, {C_out * H_out * W_out, 1, C_out * W_out, C_out}, options); dim3 grid(N, H_in, W_in); dim3 block(C_out); int smem_size = C_in * sizeof(float); AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(input.scalar_type(), "conv_transpose2d_forward", [&] { conv_transpose2d_kernel<scalar_t><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), output.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), N, H_in, W_in, C_in, KH, KW, stride, padding, dilation, H_out, W_out, C_out); }); return output; } ``` One thing: we used `zeros_strided` but need to ensure that library is available in `torch`. `torch::zeros_strided` is in libtorch. PyTorch's deterministic conv algorithms also have deterministic order, but typical conv is deterministic. So atomicAdd could fail correctness for overlapping cases. Given that, maybe we should implement gather to ensure deterministic order. Let's analyze gather performance more carefully. Gather kernel design: Grid: dim3 grid(N, H_out, W_out); Block: dim3 block(C_out); Each block computes output for one spatial pixel (n, h_out, w_out) across all c_out. Shared memory: we can load x for each valid (h_in, w_in) on demand. Since we loop over kh,kw, each iteration loads a new x vector (32 floats). We can reuse smem_x[32]. We can also load weight slice for that (kh,kw) into smem or read from global memory. Since weight reads are coalesced across threads, we can read directly from global memory (no smem needed). Kernel: ```cuda template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void conv_transpose2d_gather_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, // (N, H_in, W_in, C_in) const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, // (KH, KW, C_in, C_out) scalar_t* __restrict__ output, // (N, C_out, H_out, W_out) with NHWC strides int N, int H_in, int W_in, int C_in, int KH, int KW, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int H_out, int W_out, int C_out) { int n = blockIdx.x; int h_out = blockIdx.y; int w_out = blockIdx.z; int c_out = threadIdx.x; if (n >= N || h_out >= H_out || w_out >= W_out || c_out >= C_out) return; extern __shared__ char smem[]; scalar_t* smem_x = reinterpret_cast<scalar_t*>(smem); scalar_t acc = 0.0f; for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { int numerator_h = h_out + padding - kh * dilation; if (numerator_h % stride != 0) continue; int h_in = numerator_h / stride; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H_in) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { int numerator_w = w_out + padding - kw * dilation; if (numerator_w % stride != 0) continue; int w_in = numerator_w / stride; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W_in) continue; // Load x[n, h_in, w_in, :] into smem_x (coalesced) int in_base = ((n * H_in + h_in) * W_in + w_in) * C_in; for (int i = c_out; i < C_in; i += blockDim.x) { smem_x[i] = input[in_base + i]; } __syncthreads(); int w_base = ((kh * KW + kw) * C_in) * C_out; for (int c = 0; c < C_in; ++c) { acc += smem_x[c] * weight[w_base + c * C_out + c_out]; } __syncthreads(); } } int out_idx = ((n * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out) * C_out + c_out; output[out_idx] = acc; } ``` We load input for each valid (kh,kw) and synchronize. Since there are at most 9 valid combos, we do up to 9 loads of 32 floats and 9 dot products. This is similar to scatter but with a grid over output. For zero-output pixels, the loops over kh,kw find no valid combo, and acc remains 0. Then it writes 0 to output. This means output is overwritten with 0 for those pixels (which is correct because we zero-initialized, but we don't even need zero-initialization if all pixels are written). However, we should zero-initialize or write all pixels. With gather, we write all pixels, so we can allocate with `torch.empty_strided` and the kernel fills everything. No need for `zero_()`. That's an advantage! (Saves ~0.4ms). Shared memory size: C_in * sizeof(scalar_t) = 32*4 = 128 bytes. Negligible. Number of blocks: N*H_out*W_out = 3,246,144. Block size = 64. Total threads = 207M. This matches output size, so each thread does the minimal amount of work (collect up to 9 inputs and 9 dot products). This is highly parallelized. Since each block is small, many blocks can run concurrently. Potential issue: divergence within a warp. For a warp of 32 threads (c_out), they all execute the same h_out,w_out. For each valid (kh,kw), they all load the same x and compute the same dot product structure, just different weight element. So divergence is minimal. All threads in a block follow the same control flow for kh,kw loops. Great. Potential issue: Many blocks correspond to zero-output positions. Those blocks will quickly loop over kh,kw and find nothing (the `% stride != 0` check). But they still execute the outer loops (9 iterations). For a warp, the `continue` will cause divergence? No, all threads have same h_out,w_out, so the condition is the same across the warp. So they all continue or all proceed. No divergence. Wait, what about the condition `h_in < 0 || h_in >= H_in`? Again same for all threads in block. So no divergence. Nice. Thus the gather kernel has very uniform control flow and is safe for all parameters. Memory traffic estimates: - Input reads: For each valid (kh,kw) at each output pixel, we read C_in floats from input. Total valid output pixels = 0.36 * N*H_out*W_out ≈ 1.17M. Each has on average ~8.8 valid combos? Actually average valid combos per output pixel = total patches / total output pixels = (131072 * 8.82) / 3.25M = 0.356? Wait each input pixel contributes to ~8.8 output positions. Total contributions = 131072 * 8.82 ≈ 1.155M contributions. Each contribution loads C_in=32 floats. But with gather, each output pixel loads input for each valid (kh,kw). Number of contributions is the same 1.155M. So total input reads = 1.155M * 32 = 37M floats = 148MB. Wait we have to consider L2 cache: each input pixel is loaded multiple times? Actually each input pixel's vector is loaded once per output pixel that uses it. Since each input pixel is used by ~8.8 output pixels on average (the valid kh,kw combos), each input vector is loaded ~8.8 times. So total input traffic = 4.2M * 8.8 * 4B ≈ 148MB. Not huge. Wait, but with gather, each block reads x for its output pixel. Since many output pixels map to the same input pixel (different kh,kw), the same input pixel is read by multiple blocks. With shared memory per block, there is no reuse across blocks. So indeed each block loads the needed input vectors. Input traffic is 148MB. The host function will be something like: ```cpp torch::Tensor conv_transpose2d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int H_out, int W_out) { int N = input.size(0); int H_in = input.size(1); int W_in = input.size(2); int C_in = input.size(3); int KH = weight.size(0); int KW = weight.size(1); int C_out = weight.size(3); auto options = input.options(); std::vector<int64_t> sizes = {N, C_out, H_out, W_out}; std::vector<int64_t> strides = {C_out * H_out * W_out, 1, C_out * W_out, C_out}; auto output = torch::empty_strided(sizes, strides, options); // zero the output output.zero_(); dim3 grid(N, H_in, W_in); dim3 block(C_out); int smem_size = C_in * sizeof(float); AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(input.scalar_type(), "conv_transpose2d_forward", [&] { conv_transpose2d_kernel<scalar_t><<<grid, block, smem_size, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( input.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), output.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), N, H_in, W_in, C_in, KH, KW, stride, padding, dilation, H_out, W_out, C_out); }); return output; } ``` Wait, we need to handle C_out > 1024? Kernel: ```cuda template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void conv_transpose2d_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int H_in, int W_in, int C_in, int KH, int KW, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int H_out, int W_out, int C_out) { int n = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.z; int c_out = threadIdx.x; if (n >= N || h >= H_in || w >= W_in) return; extern __shared__ char smem[]; scalar_t* smem_x = reinterpret_cast<scalar_t*>(smem); // load input channels into smem int in_spatial = ((n * H_in + h) * W_in + w); int in_base = in_spatial * C_in; for (int i = c_out; i < C_in; i += blockDim.x) { smem_x[i] = input[in_base + i]; } __syncthreads(); // each thread handles a subset of output channels for (int co = c_out; co < C_out; co += blockDim.x) { // for each kernel spatial position for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { int h_out = h * stride + kh * dilation - padding; if (h_out < 0 || h_out >= H_out) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { int w_out = w * stride + kw * dilation - padding; if (w_out < 0 || w_out >= W_out) continue; scalar_t acc = 0.0f; int w_base = ((kh * KW + kw) * C_in) * C_out; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ++ci) { acc += smem_x[ci] * weight[w_base + ci * C_out + co]; } int out_idx = ((n * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out) * C_out + co; output[out_idx] = acc; } } } } ``` With blockDim.x = 64, each thread handles one c_out (since C_out=64). If C_out were larger, they'd loop. This kernel is robust. But we originally wanted coalesced output writes: consecutive threads writing consecutive c_out values. Weight reads: `weight[w_base + ci*C_out + co]`. For fixed ci, `co` varies across threads, contiguous addresses. Coalesced. For next ci, addresses shift by C_out. Structure of the Python file: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # Define CUDA sources cuda_source = r''' template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void conv_transpose2d_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int H_in, int W_in, int C_in, int KH, int KW, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int H_out, int W_out, int C_out) { int n = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (n >= N || h >= H_in || w >= W_in) return; extern __shared__ char smem[]; scalar_t* smem_x = reinterpret_cast<scalar_t*>(smem); int in_base = ((n * H_in + h) * W_in + w) * C_in; for (int i = tid; i < C_in; i += blockDim.x) { smem_x[i] = input[in_base + i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int co = tid; co < C_out; co += blockDim.x) { for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { int h_out = h * stride + kh * dilation - padding; if (h_out < 0 || h_out >= H_out) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { int w_out = w * stride + kw * dilation - padding; if (w_out < 0 || w_out >= W_out) continue; scalar_t acc = static_cast<scalar_t>(0.0); int w_base = ((kh * KW + kw) * C_in) * C_out; #pragma unroll for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ++ci) { acc += smem_x[ci] * weight[w_base + ci * C_out + co]; } int out_idx = ((n * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out) * C_out + co; output[out_idx] = acc; } } } } ''' ``` Wait, I should unroll the `ci` loop if possible. `#pragma unroll` with `C_in` known at compile time? `C_in` is a runtime variable. `#pragma unroll` on a runtime variable doesn't unroll. Now, the host wrapper: ```cpp torch::Tensor conv_transpose2d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int H_out, int W_out) { int N = input.size(0); int H_in = input.size(1); int W_in = input.size(2); int C_in = input.size(3); int KH = weight.size(0); int KW = weight.size(1); int C_out = weight.size(3); auto options = input.options(); std::vector<int64_t> sizes = {N, C_out, H_out, W_out}; std::vector<int64_t> strides = {C_out * H_out * W_out, 1, C_out * W_out, C_out}; auto output = torch::empty_strided(sizes, strides, options); output.zero_(); dim3 grid(N, H_in, W_in); dim3 block(64); // can tune int smem_size = C_in * sizeof(float); AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(input.scalar_type(), "conv_transpose2d_forward", [&] { conv_transpose2d_kernel<scalar_t><<<grid, block, smem_size, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( input.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), output.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), N, H_in, W_in, C_in, KH, KW, stride, padding, dilation, H_out, W_out, C_out); }); return output; } ``` But we need to ensure `block` size doesn't exceed C_out. If C_out < 64, using block=64 will cause some threads to be idle in the co loop, which is fine. But for C_out > 64, block=64 means each thread loops over co = tid, tid+64, ... up to C_out. That's fine but writes are coalesced within each iteration. However, if C_out were not a multiple of 64, there might be tail. Works fine. But for our case C_out=64, perfect. Adding bias with shape (C_out,) and broadcasting to (1,C_out,1,1) would add to the channel dimension, which is contiguous in the tensor metadata (stride 1). In our physical NHWC layout, the channel dimension is indeed contiguous (stride 1). So `out + bias.view(1,-1,1,1)` correctly adds bias to each channel at all spatial positions. Great! Because our custom strides make channel the innermost physical dimension. So PyTorch broadcasting works correctly. When broadcasting, the expanded strides of bias become (0,1,0,0) for shape (N,C,H,W). The output stride for bias dimension is 1. So elementwise addition will iterate over C dimension contiguously, H and W with stride C*W and C. This matches our out strides. So yes, it works. Thus we can handle bias easily. Now, atomicAdd on float: `atomicAdd` is built-in for float in CUDA. For double, `atomicAdd` is available on sm >= 60. Since we compile for sm_90, fine. Potential hazard: the auto-generated pybind wrapper might need to know the function signature, and it should be in a parseable source. Placing it in `cuda_sources` is okay because the wrapper scans source text via regex maybe. Let's verify by compiling. Let's produce final code. One caveat: if hidden test uses CPU tensor, we fall back to PyTorch conv_transpose2d. That's fine. Now code. ```python cuda_source= r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void conv_transpose2d_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int H_in, int W_in, int C_in, int KH, int KW, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int H_out, int W_out, int C_out) { int n = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.z; if (n >= N || h >= H_in || w >= W_in) return; extern __shared__ char smem[]; scalar_t* smem_x = reinterpret_cast<scalar_t*>(smem); int in_offset = ((n * H_in + h) * W_in + w) * C_in; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C_in; i += blockDim.x) { smem_x[i] = input[in_offset + i]; } __syncthreads(); // Precompute h_out offsets for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { int h_out = h * stride + kh * dilation - padding; if (h_out < 0 || h_out >= H_out) continue; int h_out_offset = (n * H_out + h_out) * W_out; for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { int w_out = w * stride + kw * dilation - padding; if (w_out < 0 || w_out >= W_out) continue; int out_offset = (h_out_offset + w_out) * C_out; int w_base = ((kh * KW + kw) * C_in) * C_out; for (int co = threadIdx.x; co < C_out; co += blockDim.x) { scalar_t val = static_cast<scalar_t>(0.0); // unroll? let compiler handle for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ++ci) { val += smem_x[ci] * weight[w_base + ci * C_out + co]; } atomicAdd(&output[out_offset + co], val); } } } } torch::Tensor conv_transpose2d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int H_out, int W_out) { int N = input.size(0); int H_in = input.size(1); int W_in = input.size(2); int C_in = input.size(3); int KH = weight.size(0); int KW = weight.size(1); int C_out = weight.size(3); auto output = torch::zeros({N, C_out, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); dim3 grid(N, H_in, W_in); int block_size = 128; int smem_size = C_in * sizeof(float); AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(input.scalar_type(), "conv_transpose2d_forward", [&] { conv_transpose2d_kernel<scalar_t><<<grid, block_size, smem_size, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( input.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), output.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), N, H_in, W_in, C_in, KH, KW, stride, padding, dilation, H_out, W_out, C_out); }); return output; } ''' ``` Potential issue: block_size = 128; if C_out < 128, some threads idle for co loop. Let's test with just `cuda_source` containing everything and `cpp_sources` containing just the forward declaration. Let's also revisit the algorithm correctness: transpose conv. PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d with `groups=1` is equivalent to a dense convolution between input and weight where weight shape is (C_out x C_in x K x K) but the operation is convolutionally transposed... Actually mathematically, the transposed convolution with square kernel and no groups equals: For each output position (n, c_out, h_out, w_out), the value is sum_{c_in, kh, kw} input[n, c_in, h_in, w_in] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw], where h_in and w_in satisfy: h_out = h_in * stride + kh * dilation - padding. Only specific (h_in, kh) and (w_in, kw) contribute. Equivalently, for each input element at (n, c_in, h, w) and each kernel element (kh, kw), it contributes to output at (n, :, h_out, w_out) = dot product of input value * weight[:, c_in, kh, kw]. In terms of weight layout in PyTorch: `self.weight` shape is `[C_out, C_in, K, K]`. So for given `c_in`, `kh`, `kw`, the vector of weights over `c_out` is `self.weight[:, c_in, kh, kw]`. In my kernel I ordered weight as `w_perm = weight.permute(2, 3, 0, 1)` -> shape `[K, K, C_out, C_in]`. Wait, earlier I had `w_base = ((kh * KW + kw) * C_in) * C_out` and then `weight[w_base + ci * C_out + co]`. This means weight layout is `[K, K, C_in, C_out]` after permute. Let's check what PyTorch's weight dimension order is. PyTorch ConvTranspose2d weight shape: `[in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size[0], kernel_size[1]]`. That is `[C_in, C_out, K, K]`. We want for each `kh, kw`, each `c_in` to get weights across `c_out`: that's `self.weight[:, :, kh, kw]`? Actually `self.weight[c_in, c_out, kh, kw]`. For a fixed `(c_in, kh, kw)`, the weights across `c_out` are `self.weight[c_in, :, kh, kw]`. So a slice of shape `[C_out]`. If I permute `self.weight` to `[K, K, C_in, C_out]`, then indexing `perm[kh, kw, c_in, c_out]` gives `self.weight[c_in, c_out, kh, kw]`. Correct. So `w_perm = weight.permute(2, 3, 0, 1).contiguous()` gives `[K, K, C_in, C_out]`. In my kernel, `w_base = ((kh * KW + kw) * C_in) * C_out` and then `weight[w_base + ci * C_out + co]` indexes this contiguous block correctly. Then `val += smem_x[ci] * weight[...]` means `val[co] += input[ci] * weight[ci, co]`. That matches the formula: output[co] = sum_{ci} input[ci] * weight[ci, co], where weight is `self.weight[c_in, c_out, kh, kw]`. Yes, this is correct. But wait, in my earlier test: `nn.ConvTranspose2d(1,1,3,stride=5,padding=1,dilation=2)` gives weight shape [1,1,3,3]. After permute [3,3,1,1]. x_perm is [1,1,1,1]. The kernel does: for h=0,w=0 (only one input pixel). Shared mem x[0] = 1. Then for kh=0..2, kw=0..2, calculates h_out, w_out. For kh=1,kw=1 (center), h_out = 0*5 + 1*2 - 1 = 1, w_out=1. w_base = ((1*3+1)*1)*1 = 4. weight[4] = 1. val = 1*1 = 1. atomicAdd output[1,1] = 1. For other kh,kw positions, output is zero because h_out or w_out not in [0,2]. So result is a 3x3 tensor with only center=1. That matched reference. So formula is correct. Now about atomicAdd: In original PyTorch output, each output pixel can receive contributions from multiple input positions. For example, with overlapping regions. In my earlier example there was no overlap because input was 1x1, but in general there is. AtomicAdd is necessary if multiple input pixels write to the same output pixel. However, atomicAdd is slow, especially fp32 on recent GPUs. We may need a more sophisticated reduction to avoid atomics, but let's first get correctness working. But wait, in the case of no overlap (stride >= effective kernel size), there is no atomic contention and we don't even need atomicAdd. ``` `cuda_sources`: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void conv_transpose2d_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int H_in, int W_in, int C_in, int KH, int KW, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int H_out, int W_out, int C_out) { int n = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.z; if (n >= N || h >= H_in || w >= W_in) return; extern __shared__ char smem[]; scalar_t* smem_x = reinterpret_cast<scalar_t*>(smem); int in_offset = ((n * H_in + h) * W_in + w) * C_in; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C_in; i += blockDim.x) { smem_x[i] = input[in_offset + i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { int h_out = h * stride + kh * dilation - padding; if (h_out < 0 || h_out >= H_out) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { int w_out = w * stride + kw * dilation - padding; if (w_out < 0 || w_out >= W_out) continue; int w_base = ((kh * KW + kw) * C_in) * C_out; #pragma unroll for (int co = threadIdx.x; co < C_out; co += blockDim.x) { scalar_t val = static_cast<scalar_t>(0.0); #pragma unroll for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ++ci) { val += smem_x[ci] * weight[w_base + ci * C_out + co]; } int out_offset = ((n * C_out + co) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out; output[out_offset] = val; } } } } torch::Tensor conv_transpose2d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int H_out, int W_out) { int N = input.size(0); int H_in = input.size(1); int W_in = input.size(2); int C_in = input.size(3); int KH = weight.size(0); int KW = weight.size(1); int C_out = weight.size(3); auto output = torch::zeros({N, C_out, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); dim3 grid(N, H_in, W_in); int block_size = 64; int smem_size = C_in * sizeof(float); AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(input.scalar_type(), "conv_transpose2d_forward", [&] { conv_transpose2d_kernel<scalar_t><<<grid, block_size, smem_size>>>( input.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), output.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), N, H_in, W_in, C_in, KH, KW, stride, padding, dilation, H_out, W_out, C_out); }); return output; } ``` Then `functions=['conv_transpose2d_forward']`. Can we avoid atomicAdd in general? There are a few strategies: 1. Use a work distribution where each output pixel is computed by one thread, but that requires iterating over all input pixels that map to it, which is the transpose of the usual convolution. This is actually how cuDNN implements conv transpose sometimes. Each output pixel (n, c_out, h_out, w_out) receives contributions from all (kh, kw) and corresponding (h_in, w_in). This can be computed without atomics because each thread writes to a unique output pixel. Let's analyze this. For a given output position (h_out, w_out), which input pixels and kernel elements contribute? Transposed convolution: output is sum_{kh, kw} x[h_out - kh*dilation + padding, w_out - kw*dilation + padding] * W[kh, kw] (with appropriate stride handling). Actually, the mapping isn't that simple because of stride. But conceptually, each output pixel is a dot product between a slice of the (upsampled) input and the flipped kernel. More concretely: in conv transpose, you can think of placing the kernel at positions determined by the input pixels. An output pixel (h_out, w_out) is influenced by input pixels (h_in, w_in) such that: h_out = h_in * stride + kh * dilation - padding for some kh in [0, K-1]. So h_in = floor((h_out + padding - kh*dilation) / stride). For each kh, if the division is exact (i.e., (h_out + padding - kh*dilation) % stride == 0), then there is a contributing h_in. In general, there can be at most K such input pixels per dimension, but with stride > 1, usually at most 1 or a few. Actually, the number of contributing input pixels is equal to the number of kernel positions that map exactly. This is similar to a sparse convolution. So, if we launch one thread per output pixel, we can compute its value by iterating over kh and kw, checking if the corresponding h_in and w_in are integers and within bounds, and accumulating. This completely avoids atomics because each thread writes to a unique memory location. This is the better approach! It also preserves memory layout (NCHW) naturally, or we can write directly in NCHW. That might be a lot, but GPUs can handle millions of blocks (though it might be heavy). But each thread only does a small number of computations: at most K*K = 9 iterations, each with a dot product of C_in * C_out? Wait, if we assign one thread per output pixel, we need to compute the value for all C_out channels simultaneously or one thread per (n, h, w, co). That's 207M threads. That's actually fine? Let's calculate: 207M threads / 128 threads per block = ~1.6M blocks. On a GPU with 128 SMs, each SM can handle maybe 32 blocks concurrently = 4096 blocks at a time. So 1.6M blocks would take ~400 waves. Each thread does ~9 * 32 FMAs = 288 FMAs. Total FLOPs = 207M * 288 ~ 60B FMAs. That is compute-bound. Let's compute memory: reading input for each output pixel is not shared; but each input pixel is read many times (by all output pixels it influences). With stride=5, each input pixel influences 9 output pixels. So total input reads = 16*32*64*128 * 9 * 32? Actually input elements read: each output pixel reads at most 9 input pixels (one per kh,kw) of C_in channels. So total input element reads = 207M * 9 * 32 = 60B reads. That's huge memory traffic (60B * 4 bytes = 240 GB). Weight reads: each thread reads K*K*C_in weights? Actually the weights are shared across threads. If we don't cache weights, each thread reads 9*32 floats = 288 floats = 1.1KB. Total weight reads = 207M * 1.1KB = enormous. This approach is not efficient due to unshared memory accesses. It is better to assign one block per output spatial location and have threads compute across channels, reusing weights from shared memory or caches. But avoiding atomics is still possible if we process output pixels in a way that ensures disjoint writes. It didn't reach import stage because compile failed. Then attempt 3 used `cpp_source` + `cuda_source` and gave import error `CXXABI_1.3.15 not found`. That was because it compiled and attempted to load without ABI flags. That means the compiled .so required a newer libstdc++ than available. Then attempt 4 added `-D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0` but got undefined symbol because torch expects new ABI. So torch package in this environment was compiled with new ABI (`_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=1`), but the system libstdc++ is too old (doesn't have CXXABI_1.3.15). This is a classic mismatch. It likely means there is a newer libstdc++ somewhere else (e.g., in `/opt/conda/lib` or in a pip package directory) that we need to load. If we don't set the ABI flag, the compiled module will link against whatever libstdc++ the compiler uses (likely the system one) which is too old, causing import error. But if we set the new ABI flag explicitly (i.e., `_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=1` which is default), it may still link against the old system lib at load time, causing `CXXABI_1.3.15` not found. However, we can force linking to a newer libstdc++ via `-Wl,-rpath,/path/to/new/lib`. Let's inspect: `torch.utils.cpp_extension` typically picks the compiler from environment. In this case `/opt/conda/bin/x86_64-conda-linux-gnu-c++`. That compiler presumably ships with its own libstdc++ in `/opt/conda/lib`. The issue is that the runtime dynamic linker uses `/lib/x86_64-linux-gnu/libstdc++.so.6` because it's earlier in the search path. We can add `-Wl,-rpath,/opt/conda/lib` to embed the path in the .so, so it finds conda's libstdc++ first. This should allow us to compile with the default ABI (new ABI, matching torch) and load successfully. Let's test by adding `extra_ldflags=['-Wl,-rpath,/opt/conda/lib']`.

The `Ss` means it's compiled with older ABI (libstdc++ < C++11 ABI uses `std::string` as `Ss`). PyTorch built with `_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=1` uses `std::__cxx11::basic_string` (which would mangle differently, maybe `NSt7__cxx1112basic_stringIcSt11char_traitsIcESaIcEEE`). The error specifically is undefined symbol `_ZN3c105ErrorC2ENS_14SourceLocationESs`. This is the constructor `c10::Error::Error(c10::SourceLocation, std::string)` in the old ABI. It fails because the available `c10::Error` is new ABI `c10::Error::Error(c10::SourceLocation, std::__cxx11::basic_string...)`. If PyTorch was compiled with new ABI, and our extension was compiled with old ABI, it expects old-ABI symbol and doesn't find it. So we need to match PyTorch's ABI. Our old-ABI extension tries to reference `c10::Error::Error(SourceLocation, std::string)` (old ABI) but that symbol doesn't exist because PyTorch uses new ABI string. So our extension ABI should match PyTorch: `1`. The default for the compiler using `-std=c++17` might be new ABI. We only got undefined symbol when we forced `0`.
